                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="f4a0ae6d6b0f46a0abfa0bbb9fb430a1"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="f4a0ae6d6b0f46a0abfa0bbb9fb430a1">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '5cb6392e-3785-4fed-850e-8bd0cb1329e6'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                        <li class="sm-account__item"><a class="sm-account__link" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:yuliu@mail.sic.ac.cn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-ic_mail_solid"></i><span>yuliu@mail.sic.ac.cn</span></a></li>
                     </ul>
                     <ul class="rlist sm-account">
                        <li class="sm-account__item"><a target="_blank" class="sm-account__link" href="https://orcid.org/0000-0002-7261-455X"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-orcid"></i><span>orcid.org/0000-0002-7261-455X</span></a></li>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d64193286" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Despite their low cost and high specific capacity, the practical use of monoclinic Mn-based Prussian blue analogs (Mn-PBAs) is limited by poor cycling stability, primarily due to the structural degradation caused by the multiple phase transitions and Jahn–Teller effect associated with Mn<sup>3+</sup> ions throughout the entire cycle. Herein, by synergistic incorporation of low-cost Cu and Fe into the manganese sites of Mn-PBAs, the ternary PBAs (T-PBAs) achieve solid solution reaction of Na<sup>+</sup> extraction and insertion, successfully eliminating the inherent multi-phase transition from the sodium storage mechanism of T-PBAs. Ex situ analysis and density functional theory calculations are employed to confirm that T-PBAs consistently maintain a cubic phase with smaller lattice distortion rather than occur in conventional three-phase transition during the charging and discharging processes, forcefully inhibiting the structural degradation of T-PBAs. Therefore, the T-PBAs showcase unprecedented cycling stability at both room temperature (10&nbsp;000 cycles at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and −20&nbsp;°C (over 3000&nbsp;h, 4200 cycles at 0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> without distinct capacity degradation), More importantly, when paired with commercial hard carbon, the T-PBAs based sodium-ion batteries exhibit excellent capacity retention (2000 cycles 76.8%), showcasing their immense potential in practical applications.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202406809-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202406809-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Considering the clear benefits derived from the plentiful availability of sodium resources, alongside their analogous operational mechanisms, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are recognized as a viable substitute for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) within the context of extensive energy storage solutions. The principal obstacle facing SIBs is the lack of advanced cathode materials with high specific capacity, fast charge/discharge capability, and long lifespans.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0001_R_d64193143e387" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Three kinds of cathodes have been widely studied, including layered oxides,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0002_R_d64193143e392" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Sci.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> polyanionic compounds,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0003_R_d64193143e397" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Small">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Prussian blue analogs (PBAs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0004_R_d64193143e403" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - J. Mater. Chem. A">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, PBAs are recognized as one of the most promising cathodes for commercial SIBs attribute to their rapid sodium diffusion channels, high theoretical specific capacity, and low production costs. The general chemical formula of PBAs could be denoted as Na<sub>2-x</sub>M[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>1-y</sub>□y▪nH<sub>2</sub>O, where M represents transition elements and □ is [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies (0 &lt; x ≤ 2, 0 ≤ y &lt; 1). Based on the number of redox-active sites when utilized as electrode materials for SIBs, PBAs can be classified into two types: the single-electron transfer type (M = Zn, Ni, etc.) and the dual-electron transfer type (M = Mn, Fe, Co, etc.) with maximum capacities of 85 and 170&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0005_R_d64193143e421" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Appealed by the high redox potential and electrochemical activity of manganese (Mn) elements, the Na-rich monoclinic phase Mn-based PBAs (Mn-PBAs) are typically viewed as the leading contenders in the PBAs category. However, Mn-PBAs suffer from poor cycling reversibility and capacity retention in actual use, which is primarily due to the dissolution of Mn in the electrolytes and structural distortion caused by the Jahn–Teller effect associated with oxidized Mn<sup>3+</sup> ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0006_R_d64193143e430" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, monoclinic phase Mn-PBAs will undergo unfavorable multiple phase transitions throughout the entire cycle (monoclinic ↔ cubic ↔ tetragonal) along with serious lattice distortion, which would cause the accumulation of local fracture and degradation,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0007_R_d64193143e435" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. Nat. Energy">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> thus resulting in the generation of microcracks and damaged particles. These processes trigger a cascade of harmful effects on Mn-PBAs cathodes, which includes the chemical corrosion/dissolution from surface to bulk, the formation and growth of new cathode electrolyte interlayer (CEI) in the intergranular cracks, and continuous impedance growth and structure degradation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0008_R_d64193143e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Small Methods Adv. Energy Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Numerous efforts have been dedicated to improving the cycle life of Mn-PBAs cathodes, including doping with structural enhancement elements such as Ni<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0009_R_d64193143e446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and incorporating electrochemically active elements like Co. The latter is aimed at partially replacing Mn to alleviate the Jahn–Teller effect.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0010_R_d64193143e451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, these strategies generally suffer from similar issues: 1) the integration of electrochemically inactive components tends to reduce the specific capacity of Mn-PBAs; 2) incorporating precious metals such as cobalt and nickel significantly elevates the manufacturing costs of PBAs. In summary, while these methods improve the cyclic stability of Mn-PBAs, they contradict the fundamental attributes of high theoretical specific capacity and low production costs associated with PBAs. Therefore, identifying cost-effective strategies to mitigate the structural degradation of Mn-PBAs is crucial for advancing their practical applications.</p>
         
         <p>Solid-solution reactions are pivotal in the realm of solid-state reactions, finding extensive applications within the fields of materials science and chemical engineering. Pertinent research on layered oxides in SIBs demonstrates how manipulating the electron population to control the reaction path, through the synergistic substitution of transition metal (TM) ions, can enhance electron hybridization. This, in turn, directs the system toward a thermodynamically favorable solid solution pathway, effectively replacing the conventional two-phase process. By circumventing the formation of phase boundaries, severe localized strain, and cracks, this approach yields exceptional cycling stability and rate performance in otherwise unstable oxides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0011_R_d64193143e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Inspired by this concept, we have pioneered the use of solid-solution reactions to enhance the cycling stability of Mn-PBAs. Departing from the traditional monoclinic phase Mn-PBAs, cubic-phase ternary PBAs (T-PBAs) were successfully prepared by synergistic incorporation of low-cost Cu and Fe into the manganese sites of Mn-PBAs, which achieving solid solution reaction of Na<sup>+</sup> extraction and insertion at charging/discharging process. Ex situ analysis and density functional theory (DFT) calculations were employed to confirm that T-PBAs consistently maintain a cubic phase with smaller lattice distortion rather than undergoing conventional three-phase transition during the charging and discharging processes. As a result, the solid solution reaction effectively inhibits the structural degradation of T-PABs, thereby enabling it to achieve an ultralong lifespan (10&nbsp;000 cycle at 1A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). Meanwhile, the T-PBAs can also deliver a high specific capacity of 123&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (≈400&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>), because all elements involved exhibit redox activity during the charging and discharging processes. Such a long lifespan coupled with the high capacity, to our best knowledge, represents a significant breakthrough among the reports on nonaqueous PBAs. Additionally, the T-PBAs can be stably cycled at −20&nbsp;°C for over 3000&nbsp;h (4200 cycles at 0.2A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) without distinct capacity degradation, stored in air for 3 months without any degradation of electrochemical performance, and stably cycling for &gt;2000 cycles when coupled with pre-sodiated (hard carbon) HC to form full-cell. All of the above merits convincingly suggest the huge application potential of T-PBAs for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202406809-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202406809-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202406809-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202406809-sec-0030-title">2.1 Materials Characterizations</h3>
            
            <p>The Materials of T-PBAs, FeMn-PBAs, and CuMn-PBAs were synthesized via a co-precipitation method (Supporting Information). According to the XRD patterns in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a> (Supporting Information), the split peaks observed at 24.2°, 38.65°, and 49.53° indicate that FeMn-PBAs and CuMn-PBAs are the monoclinic phases while the presence of single peaks at these angles show that the T-PBAs possess cubic phase. Such phase difference is considered to be caused by sodium content.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0012_R_d64193143e492" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Further analysis through XRD Rietveld refinement (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0001">1a</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> b,c and Tables&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1–S3</a>, Supporting Information) shows that the calculated lattice parameters of T-PBAs are a&nbsp;=&nbsp;b&nbsp;=&nbsp;c&nbsp;=&nbsp;10.295&nbsp;Å, α&nbsp;=&nbsp;β&nbsp;=&nbsp;γ&nbsp;=&nbsp;90° with space group Fm-3 m, while the space group of the other two PBAs is identified as P 21/n.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0013_R_d64193143e510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Storage Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to the Raman spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a</a>, Supporting Information), the two peaks at ≈2100 and 2130&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> are assigned to the Fe<sup>2+</sup>-CN-M<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup>-CN-M<sup>3+</sup>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0014_R_d64193143e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nano Energy">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The higher Fe<sup>2+</sup>-CN-M<sup>2+</sup> peak for CuMn(FeMn)-PBAs also confirms its higher sodium content than that of T-PBAs. Therefore, the crystalline phase of PBAs can also be determined to some extent by analyzing Raman peaks.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0015_R_d64193143e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nano Energy Adv. Funct. Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> What's more, the structure was also examined by Fourier Transform Infrared Spectroscopy (FTIR) (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2b</a>, Supporting Information). The peak at 2075&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> can be attributed to the stretching vibration of the C≡N group coordinated by Fe<sup>2+</sup>-C≡N-M<sup>2+</sup> and the sharp peak at ≈3611&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> is attributed to the ν(OH) of ligand H<sub>2</sub>O.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0016_R_d64193143e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The chemical formulas of all the samples are listed in Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information), e.g., Na<sub>1.59</sub>Mn<sub>0.42</sub>Cu<sub>0.18</sub>Fe<sub>0.76</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]⚫2.74H<sub>2</sub>O of T-PBAs according to the inductively coupled plasma—optical emission spectroscopy (ICP-OES) results and EA of CHN analysis. Furthermore, the as-synthesized T-PBAs possess cubic morphology, with average edge lengths spanning 1–3&nbsp;µm. Such decent and uniform particle size significantly simplifies the subsequent separation and coating processes. Selected Area Electron Diffraction (SAED) and High-Resolution Transmission Electron Microscopy (HRTEM) analyses were conducted to further investigate the crystal structure of T-PBAs. As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0001">1e,f</a>, the (200)/(220) planes were readily identifiable within the cubic structure of T-PBAs, which is consistent with the XRD results. The distribution of all the elements in T-PBAs is verified by SEM energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mapping images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0001">1g</a>), confirming the uniform distribution of the elements in the particles.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202406809-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2b95b4a1-896c-49e8-b9eb-5214c1748ca6/adfm202406809-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/2b95b4a1-896c-49e8-b9eb-5214c1748ca6/adfm202406809-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/79dd2481-78a0-4c76-9cf4-2febccedcd01/adfm202406809-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2b95b4a1-896c-49e8-b9eb-5214c1748ca6/adfm202406809-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202406809-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202406809" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The Rietveld refinement of the XRD pattern; b) The cubic crystal structure model; c) SEM image; d) TEM image; e) SAED pattern; f) HRTEM images; g) EDS element mapping of T-PBAs.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202406809-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202406809-sec-0040-title">2.2 Half-Cell Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The sodium-ion storage performance of all the electrodes was investigated within 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V using sodium metal as the counter electrode. As illustrated in <b>Figures</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0002">2a,b</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a,b</a> (Supporting Information), the low plateau (≈2.7–3.0&nbsp;V) observed for T-PBAs and FeMn-PBAs is associated with the redox reactions of high-spin iron, whereas CuMn-PBAs exhibits only a singular redox plateau spanning 3.0–3.5&nbsp;V. Nonetheless, the specific capacities of all cathode materials are closely matched, indicating that the doped transition metals (TM) exhibit comparable redox activities. It's worth noting that the initial coulombic efficiency of T(FeMn)-PBAs is higher than 100%, possibly due to oxidation of some of the Fe during synthesis. The specific capacity of CuMn(FeMn)-PBAs exhibits a gradual increase over the initial five cycles, in contrast to the stability observed in T-PBAs. This trend may be due to the lattice expansion resulting from the embedded detachment of sodium ions, which underscores the structural stability of T-PBAs to some extent.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0017_R_d64193143e623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Storage Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Cyclic Voltammetry (CV) spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0002">2c</a>) reveal that T-PBAs feature two pairs of redox peaks during charging and discharging, corresponding to the two distinct discharge plateaus. The oxidation/reduction peaks at ≈3.10/2.8&nbsp;V correspond to the Fe<sup>2+/3+</sup> redox pairs of high-spin (HS) Fe linked with N,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0018_R_d64193143e634" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the redox peaks of HS Cu<sup>1+/2+</sup>, Mn<sup>2+/3+</sup> and LS Fe<sup>2+/3+</sup> merged to become a single broad peak at 3.54/3.37&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0019_R_d64193143e646" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the rate capability of both cathodes was measured at various current rates ranging from 0.02 to 6.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0002">2d</a>). The T-PBAs display a discharge capacity of ≈123.0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at a low current density of 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Remarkably, even under an ultra-high current density of 6.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, it sustains a capacity of 78.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, equating to ≈64.0% retention of the initial capacity observed at 0.02&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Notably, upon reverting the current density to 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the discharge capacity of the T-PBAs shows no significant degradation, highlighting the superior reversibility of the extraction/insertion reactions. In contrast, CuMn-PBAs and FeMn-PBAs only deliver capacities of 59.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and 69.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, at the high current density of 6.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202406809-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d5f876a3-d629-436d-bcee-2ef930c9b6b5/adfm202406809-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d5f876a3-d629-436d-bcee-2ef930c9b6b5/adfm202406809-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4b2cfb12-09d4-4c87-86c8-00e2bd19d9b5/adfm202406809-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d5f876a3-d629-436d-bcee-2ef930c9b6b5/adfm202406809-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202406809-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202406809" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The second charge–discharge curves of all the samples at 0.02A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>; b) The first five charge–discharge curves of T-PBAs at 0.02A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>; c) CV curves of T-PBAs at 0.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> d) Rate performance of all the cathodes; e) Calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient; f) Contour plots of CV patterns for T-PBAs with different scan rates. g) Log (I) versus log (V) plots from the CV results; h) Cycling performance of the samples at 0.5A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>; i) Mid-value voltage of the samples; j) Long-term cycling performance of T-PBAs at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 10 cycles at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>; k) Comparison of capacities and cycling performances of various electrodes.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="paragraph-element">To explore the distinctive nature of the superior rate capability of T-PBAs, galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) and CV measurements under various current rates were performed to assess the electronic/ionic diffusion efficiencies of both cathodes. Based on the GITT measurements, the insets of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0002">2e</a> clearly depict that the average <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/6a725e16-4957-4b8a-881f-927844bb07bb/adfm202406809-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202406809:adfm202406809-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202406809-math-0001.png">

Besides, the <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/013c8950-5c10-4ce7-8205-65f775cc034d/adfm202406809-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202406809:adfm202406809-math-0002" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202406809-math-0002.png">

                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$D_{Na}^ + \ $</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> as the voltage exceeds 3.45&nbsp;V, which may be due to the significant boundary effects. The higher and more stable <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/4dfa68c2-e3f3-414b-ab9e-e3de56ebf53e/adfm202406809-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202406809:adfm202406809-math-0004" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202406809-math-0004.png">

                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$D_{Na}^ + \ $</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> favors elimination of Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering in T-PBAs during charge and discharge.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0020_R_d64193143e789" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Solid State Ionics Nano Energy">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, according to the Contour plots of CV results (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0003">3f</a>) and Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202406809-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>), the b value could be calculated by linear fitting from the plot of log (i) versus log (ν)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0021_R_d64193143e804" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J.                         </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202406809-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5a2fe24e-f51c-483c-b6af-8c37ee758bcf/adfm202406809-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5a2fe24e-f51c-483c-b6af-8c37ee758bcf/adfm202406809-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ccd9488d-10a6-49ef-8e98-6079b00ac47d/adfm202406809-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5a2fe24e-f51c-483c-b6af-8c37ee758bcf/adfm202406809-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202406809-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202406809" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Ex situ XRD patterns collected during the first charge/discharge of the a,b) T-PBAs/Na half-cell; c,d) FeMn-PBAs/Na half-cell under 10&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> over a voltage range of 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V; e) unit volume variations during 1st charge–discharge process; f) Illustration of the structural evolution; g) Ex situ Raman 3D colormap surface with projection; XRD patterns of h) T-PBAs; i) FeMn-PBAs; j) CuMn-PBAs after different cycles.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The parameter “b” elucidates the sodium storage mechanism: a diffusion-limited process is indicated by a value of b&nbsp;=&nbsp;0.5, whereas a surface-limited process corresponds to b&nbsp;=&nbsp;1.0. As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0003">3g</a>, the b values associated with the redox peaks of T-PBAs are 0.82, 0.95, 0.94, and 0.90, respectively. These values are closer to 1.0 than to 0.5, signifying that the sodium storage kinetics of T-PBAs are primarily governed by surface-limited reactions. Furthermore, the CV test at different scan rates was also applied to identify the Na<sup>+</sup> migration kinetics of the individual redox pairs in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> and Tables&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7–S9</a> (Supporting Information). As for T-PBAs, values of 3.37389&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−10</sup> and 2.7984&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−10</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> are obtained for the 1st and 4th peaks, respectively<b>—</b>about an order of magnitude higher than those obtained in FeMn(CuMn)-PBAs. The sluggish diffusion of FeMn(CuMn)-PBAs in the low-charging platform may be caused by phase changes corresponding to monoclinic ↔ cubic. Conversely, the solid solution reaction in the low charging platform prevents phase transitions, thereby ensuring the rapid sodium ion diffusion for T-PBAs.</p>
            
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0002">2h</a> illustrates the cycle stability of various electrodes at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Among them, T-PBAs stand out for their superior cycling performance, demonstrating an initial capacity of ≈108&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and maintaining a capacity of ≈96&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> even after 1500 cycles (with a minimal capacity decay rate of only 0.76% per 100 cycles), highlighting its exceptional durability compared to the other two cathodes. This pronounced difference may be due to the more significant structural degradation experienced by the FeMn(CuMn)-PBAs. To demonstrate its superiority for practical applications, we have conducted tests on its performance at low temperatures and evaluated its long-term stability in the air. The comparison of XRD patterns (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7a</a>, Supporting Information) between the fresh powder and the powder exposed to air for three months reveals no discernible peak shifts or variations, confirming the stability of the T-PBAs in ambient conditions. Conceivably, there is no surprise that the rate performance and long-term cycling stability of the air-exposed T-PBAs sample are almost the same as those of the fresh sample in consideration of the unchanged crystal structure (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7b,c</a>, Supporting Information). Additionally, T-PBAs can be stably cycled at −20&nbsp;°C for over 3000&nbsp;h (4200 cycles at 0.2A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) without distinct capacity degradation (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7d</a>, Supporting Information). Last but not least, an unprecedented ultra-long-term cycling stability of T-PBAs is also visible (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0002">2j</a>). Following 10&nbsp;000 cycles at 1.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, they maintain a capacity of 61.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with a coulombic efficiency of ≈99.96%. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0002">2k</a> (the vertical axis represents the initial discharge capacity), T-PBAs distinguish themselves from other cathodes by achieving a true equilibrium between high capacity and cycling stability, highlighting their value in practical applications.</p>
            
            <p>Ex situ XRD analysis was conducted to examine the structural evolution and gain a deeper understanding of the sodium intercalation/deintercalation behavior of T-PBAs and FeMn-PBAs. The contour maps from the ex situ XRD test of all the cathodes correspond to the charge/discharge curves in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0003">3a,d</a>, the initial potential of T-PBAs and FeMn-PBAs was measured at ≈3.0&nbsp;V instead of 2.0&nbsp;V, this observation may be due to the partial oxidation of the pole piece during the preparation process. During the initial cycle of T-PBAs, the insertion/extraction behavior of Na<sup>+</sup> ions was carefully monitored, focusing on three characteristic peaks: (200), (220), and (400) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0003">3b</a>). Throughout the first charging process, all peaks were observed to gradually shift to higher angles, indicative of volume shrinkage following the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, without any clear phase transition. Remarkably, this process is reversible, as demonstrated by the peaks returning to their original angles during the subsequent discharge process, further confirming the robust structure of T-PBAs. In contrast, for FeMn-PBAs, charging to 3.6&nbsp;V led to a pronounced shift in peak positions toward higher angles, which suggests a phase transition from cubic to tetragonal (P4/mmm space group). Additionally, during the discharge process, a shift from cubic to monoclinic phase was noted upon discharging to 3.0&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0022_R_d64193143e928" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Commun.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Such phase transformation reactions typically entail additional kinetic barriers for the initiation of a new phase and its boundary propagation, as corroborated by the calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barriers illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0002">2e</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0011_R_d64193143e942" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the electrochemical process, the peaks of T-PBAs exhibit a gradual rightward shift followed by a reversible return, indicating a solid-solution process without the production of phase transformations or boundaries. In order to better explain crystal structure change in T(FeMn)-PBAs, we calculated the cell volume of these two materials as demonstrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0003">3e</a>. Attributed to the solid-solution reaction, T-PBAs exhibit only minor lattice distortion throughout the charging and discharging cycle, in stark contrast to FeMn-PBAs, particularly during operations at 2–3&nbsp;V. It is at these voltages that FeMn-PBAs experience substantial lattice distortion, primarily caused by the monoclinic ↔ cubic phase transition.</p>
            
            <p>In consideration of the high sensitivity of the (C≡N) <sup>−</sup> vibration stretching mode to the chemical coordination environment of bonding iron and M (M: Mn, Fe, Cu), ex situ Raman spectroscopy was performed to analyze the structural changes of T-PBAs in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0003">3f,g</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information). In its pristine state, the Raman spectrum exhibits two pronounced peaks at ≈2089 and 2131&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to the Fe<sup>2+</sup>-CN-M<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup>-CN-M<sup>3+</sup> groups, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0023_R_d64193143e972" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Nano">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the initial charging process, these two initial peaks gradually decreased, accompanied by the appearance of a new peak at 2150&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, which can be ascribed to Fe<sup>3+</sup>-CN-M<sup>3+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0024_R_d64193143e984" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Remarkably, the persistence of the two initial peaks even after charging to 4.0&nbsp;V underscores that only partial oxidation of Fe and M takes place within the T-PBAs. During the discharge cycle from 4.0 to 2.0&nbsp;V, the peak at 2150&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> vanishes, with the intensities of the initial two peaks at 2089 and 2131&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> gradually restoring to their original state, indicating the highly reversible reactions of T-PBAs throughout the electrochemical cycling process. It is worth noting that even after 2000 cycles at 1A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the main diffraction peaks of T-PBAs in the XRD analysis retained their prominence. In contrast, the peak intensities of FeMn(CuMn)-PBAs showed a significant reduction when compared to their initial state (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0003">3h–j</a>). This indicates that the solid solution reaction effectively mitigates the structural degradation of T-PBAs, which in turn ensures their exceptional cycling performance.</p>
            
            <p>To delve deeper into the reaction mechanisms of each electrochemically active metal component (Mn, Fe, Cu) in T-PBAs, ex situ XPS analyses were conducted on cathodes in their pristine state and at various charge/discharge voltages. In the initial state of T-PBAs, Fe cations predominantly exist in a divalent state. Meanwhile, Mn cations are present in a mixture of divalent and trivalent states, and Cu cations exhibit a mixture of monovalent and divalent states. This variability in oxidation states may be due to unavoidable oxidation processes during the preparation or handling of the materials. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0004">4a</a>, the Mn 2p<sub>3/2</sub> peaks located at 641.4 and 642.7&nbsp;eV correspond to the Mn (II) and Mn (III), respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0023_R_d64193143e1010" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Nano">23</a>, <a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0025_R_d64193143e1010" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Chem. Commun.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon charging to 4.0&nbsp;V, there is an observed increase in the peak area associated with Mn(III), indicating a rise in Mn(III) content. However, Mn remains in a mixed state of Mn (II) and Mn (III), signifying that Mn does not undergo complete oxidation during the charging process. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0004">4b</a> displays the Fe 2p spectra throughout the charging and discharging processes. In the initial sample, the peaks are mainly located at 708.6&nbsp;eV (Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub>) and 721.5&nbsp;eV (Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub>), indicating 2+ oxidation state of Fe.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0020_R_d64193143e1023" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Solid State Ionics Nano Energy">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> And then, upon charging to 4.0&nbsp;V, the peaks observed at 710.2 and 724.0&nbsp;eV can be attributed to the oxidation of Fe (II) to Fe (III). Nevertheless, characteristic peaks of Fe(II) remained detectable, likely due to the low-spin Fe not being fully oxidized, given its redox plateau is ≈3.5–4.2&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0026_R_d64193143e1029" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Chem. Eng. J.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When discharged back to 2.0&nbsp;V, all Fe was reduced to the Fe(II) state. As for the Cu 2p spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0004">4c</a>), the Cu 2p<sub>3/2</sub> peaks at 932.6 and 935.4&nbsp;eV, alongside the Cu 2p<sub>1/2</sub> peaks at 952.5 and 955.3&nbsp;eV, signify the coexistence of Cu<sup>+</sup> and Cu<sup>2+</sup> in the initial state. Additionally, the peak observed at ≈943&nbsp;eV is identified as the shakeup satellite of the Cu 2p peaks.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0027_R_d64193143e1046" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Funct. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similar to Mn, Cu experiences only partial redox within the charge/discharge range of 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V. All elements within T-PBAs exhibit redox activity, contributing to the high specific capacity of T-PBAs. Notably, Fe, Cu, and Mn undergo only partial oxidation during the charging process, which brings several following advantages: 1) It reduces the formation of Mn (III) in a single charging cycle, thereby fundamentally mitigating the Jahn–Teller effect of the Mn<sup>3+</sup>; 2) It limits the redox depth of these elements, thus prolonging the redox lifespan of each element. As a result, the mid-voltage decay observed in T-PBAs is significantly lower compared to that of the other two electrodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0002">2i</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202406809-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b04f8b34-5b66-45b7-9fcf-a36b3727d405/adfm202406809-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b04f8b34-5b66-45b7-9fcf-a36b3727d405/adfm202406809-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/034477a7-a48d-4a6a-8b72-93d1462dbc38/adfm202406809-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b04f8b34-5b66-45b7-9fcf-a36b3727d405/adfm202406809-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202406809-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202406809" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Ex situ XPS analysis of each electrochemically active metal component: a) Mn; b) Fe; and c) Cu at initial, 4.0&nbsp;V, and 2.0&nbsp;V. d) projected density of states (PDOS) for T-PBAs; e) Change in M─N bond length during the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To further understand the superior kinetics as well as excellent cycling stability of T-PBAs from a theoretical perspective, density functional theory (DFT) calculations were conducted. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0004">4d</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a,c</a> (Supporting Information), T-PBAs exhibit a slightly smaller bandgap (1.27&nbsp;eV) compared to FeMn-PBAs (1.57&nbsp;eV) and CuMn-PBAs (1.808&nbsp;eV). The reduced bandgap of T-PBAs facilitates easier electron transitions, enhancing the electrochemical performance. This improvement is clearly demonstrated by the enhanced rate performance of electrodes, for instance. First-principle calculations were carried out to investigate the structural transformations of all the sample PBAs during the Na<sup>+</sup> desertion process, aiming to provide deeper insights into the lattice distortion experienced by the PBAs. Each unit cell comprises 256 atoms, as this study is a proof-of-concept, the ratios of transition metal elements in the model are based on the stoichiometric ratio of the starting materials. The focus was exclusively on the low-voltage plateau region, which experiences the most significant deformation, as indicated by ex situ XRD results. During this phase, FeMn(CuMn)-PBAs were observed to undergo a phase transition from monoclinic to cubic. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0004">4e</a>, attributed to the solid solution reaction, the cubic phase T-PBAs maintain low lattice distortion, with only minor changes in the bond lengths of elements upon the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. In contrast, the transition from monoclinic to cubic phase leads to a uniform reduction in the bond lengths of the transition metal elements in FeMn(CuMn)-PBAs to ≈85% of their original sizes, accompanied by a cell volume reduction of about one-third (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12b,d</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>To better understand the structural degradation of the T-PBAs, its gassing behavior was studied by DEMS.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0028_R_d64193143e1102" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Energy Rep.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As indicated by previous research, the crystal water or coordinated water will be released alongside the phase transition from monoclinic to cubic at ≈2.0–3.0&nbsp;V, which will trigger the decomposition of water, resulting in the production of H<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0029_R_d64193143e1109" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0005">5a–d</a>, T-PBAs do not generate H<sub>2</sub> in the initial state of charge attributed to the solid solution reaction. The H<sub>2</sub> evolution at high potentials and states of charge (SOC) may be caused by the release of coordinated water in the form of Na(OH<sub>2</sub>)<sup>+</sup>, due to the reduced binding energy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0019_R_d64193143e1130" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Mater.">19</a>, <a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0030_R_d64193143e1130" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It's significant to note that during the initial charging/discharging process, there was no observation of concurrent CO<sub>2</sub> (potentially formed from electrolyte hydrolysis)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0031_R_d64193143e1137" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and (CN)<sub>2</sub> (associated with the electrochemical decomposition of PBAs)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0032_R_d64193143e1145" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Energy Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> evolution, indicating the T-PBAs exhibit fewer side effects and a more durable structure compared with others. Contrasting with both the pristine and cycled electrodes displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0005">5e</a>, the T-PBAs retain their structural integrity and smooth surface morphology even after 2000 cycles. In stark contrast, the particle surface of FeMn(CuMn)-PBAs is marred by cracks and by-products accumulation. This notable difference is credited to the T-PBAs' mechanism of undergoing solid solution reactions during the charge and discharge processes. Consequently, it is subject to smaller lattice distortions and zero phase transitions, effectively mitigating structural degradation and minimizing the incidence of side reactions (as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0005">5f</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202406809-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a57fe846-2350-4590-9338-2714d04e98fc/adfm202406809-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a57fe846-2350-4590-9338-2714d04e98fc/adfm202406809-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/672526d1-1c12-4358-800e-902f658db6ef/adfm202406809-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a57fe846-2350-4590-9338-2714d04e98fc/adfm202406809-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202406809-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202406809" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–d) Gas evolution profiles for the T-PBAs as obtained via DEMS; e) SEM images of all of the electrodes before cycling and after 2000 cycles f) Schematic illustration of the solid solution reaction suppress the byproduct and generation of microcracks of PBAs; g) In-suit EIS during the first cycle of T-PBAs at 0.01&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>; h) Distribution of relaxation times in T-PBAs//Na half-cell; cathode-electrolyte interface of i) T-PBAs; j) FeMn-PBAs; j) CuMn-PBAs.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Moreover, the in situ GEIS (galvanostatic electrochemical impedance spectroscopy) technique was employed to investigate batteries at various states of charge (SOCs), providing insights into the internal mechanisms. Measurements were taken at 0.2&nbsp;V intervals. For T-PBAs, the intersection point in the middle-low frequency region with the real axis of the EIS displayed symmetrical behavior during both charging and discharging processes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0005">5g</a>), and it is ≈500&nbsp;Ω lower than that observed for FeMn(CuMn)-PBAs (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13c,e</a>, Supporting Information). To enhance the interpretation of the in situ GEIS spectra, the distribution of relaxation times (DRT) methodology was adopted. This method is advantageous for directly pinpointing the time constants associated with key electrochemical processes, significantly refining impedance analysis and greatly improving the precision of kinetic interpretations on the timescale (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0005">5h</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406809-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406809-bib-0033_R_d64193143e1190" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Electrochim. Acta Joule Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. J. Power Sources">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to previous research, the total timescale distributions of different kinetics processes of T-PBAs//Na, FeMn-PBAs//Na, and CuMn-PBAs//Na half-cell were depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0005">5i</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information). The resistance and “τ” parameter for the cathode-electrolyte interface in the T-PBAs sample is distinctly lower than those observed in the other two, which may be due to the fewer byproducts occurring on the T-PBAs, aligning with prior observations.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202406809-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202406809-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrochemical Properties of Full Cell</h3>
            
            <p>To further evaluate the possibility of practical application of T-PBAs, full cells were assembled by T-PBAs and pre-sodium HC, and the working mechanism of the designed T-PBAs//HC full cell configuration is sketched in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0006">6</a> a. As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0006">6b</a>, the T-PBAs//HC full-cells operate within a wide voltage range of 1.8–3.8&nbsp;V and a high mid-working voltage of ≈3.2&nbsp;V. At the current density of 50&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the full cell showcases a specific capacity of 107.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which corresponds to a relative specific energy of 330&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>. Remarkably, even at a high current density of 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0006">6c,d</a>), the full cell sustains a capacity of 83.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, implying its decent rate capability. The cycling performance was tested at 0.3&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406809-fig-0006">6e</a>. Even after 2000 cycles, the full cell still retains a high capacity of 76.8%, corresponding to a specific capacity of 77.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. This study decisively demonstrates the viability of T-PBAs as a promising cathode material for SIBs, heralding a sustainable path forward for large-scale application.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202406809-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/341ab314-1752-403c-928b-5fd5f5a8c146/adfm202406809-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/341ab314-1752-403c-928b-5fd5f5a8c146/adfm202406809-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7cdbc912-a322-47d7-b485-ea4e0e891fac/adfm202406809-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/341ab314-1752-403c-928b-5fd5f5a8c146/adfm202406809-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202406809-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202406809" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The schematic diagram of full-cell; b) The voltage matchup by the charge/discharge galvanostatic curves of T-PBAs//Na and HC//Na; c) The full-cell charge/discharge curves at various rates of T-PBAs//HC; d) The charge/discharge of T-PBAs/HC full-cell at various rates; e) Long-cycle performance of T-PBAs//HC full-cell (specific capacity calculated by active mass of T-PBAs).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202406809-sec-0060">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202406809-sec-0060-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In this work, the cubic-phase ternary PBAs (T-PBAs) were successfully prepared by synergistic incorporation of low-cost Cu and Fe into the manganese sites of Mn-PBAs, which achieves solid solution reaction of Na<sup>+</sup> extraction and insertion at charging/discharging process. Therefore, T-PBAs consistently maintain a cubic phase with reduced lattice distortion during sodium-ions insertion and extraction, significantly improving their cycling stability as cathode materials. As a result, T-PBAs demonstrate exceptional cycling stability at both room temperature and −20&nbsp;°C, as well as excellent air stability. The excellent electrochemical performance of full cells further validates the potential of T-PBAs for practical use in SIBs. This work could provide a fundamental guideline for the development of advanced PBAs cathode materials for grid-scale energy storage applications.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
